<p><b>Dresden</b> (<small>German pronunciation:</small> <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA/Standard_German" title="Help:IPA/Standard German">[ˈdʁeːsdn̩]</a></span><small class="nowrap metadata">&#160;(<span class="unicode haudio"><span class="fn"><span style="white-space:nowrap"><a href="/wiki/File:De-Dresden-pronunciation.ogg" title="About this sound"><img alt="About this sound" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8a/Loudspeaker.svg/11px-Loudspeaker.svg.png" width="11" height="11" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8a/Loudspeaker.svg/17px-Loudspeaker.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8a/Loudspeaker.svg/22px-Loudspeaker.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="20" data-file-height="20" /></a>&#160;</span><a href="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/0/08/De-Dresden-pronunciation.ogg" class="internal" title="De-Dresden-pronunciation.ogg">listen</a></span></span>)</small>; <a href="/wiki/Czech_language" title="Czech language">Czech</a>: <span lang="cs" xml:lang="cs"><i>Drážďany</i></span>, <a href="/wiki/Polish_language" title="Polish language">Polish</a>: <span lang="pl" xml:lang="pl"><i>Drezno</i></span>) is the capital city<sup id="cite_ref-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-2">[2]</a></sup> and, after <a href="/wiki/Leipzig" title="Leipzig">Leipzig</a>, the second-largest city<sup id="cite_ref-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-3">[3]</a></sup> of the <a href="/wiki/Free_State_of_Saxony" class="mw-redirect" title="Free State of Saxony">Free State of Saxony</a> in <a href="/wiki/Germany" title="Germany">Germany</a>. It is situated in a valley on the River <a href="/wiki/Elbe" title="Elbe">Elbe</a>, near the <a href="/wiki/Czech_Republic%E2%80%93Germany_border" title="Czech Republic–Germany border">border</a> with the <a href="/wiki/Czech_Republic" title="Czech Republic">Czech Republic</a>.</p>
<p>Dresden has a long history as the capital and royal residence for the <a href="/wiki/Prince-Elector" class="mw-redirect" title="Prince-Elector">Electors</a> and <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Saxony" title="Kingdom of Saxony">Kings of Saxony</a>, who for centuries furnished the city with cultural and artistic splendour. The city was known as the Jewel Box, because of its <a href="/wiki/Baroque" title="Baroque">baroque</a> and <a href="/wiki/Rococo" title="Rococo">rococo</a> city centre. The controversial <a href="/wiki/Bombing_of_Dresden_in_World_War_II" title="Bombing of Dresden in World War II">American and British bombing of Dresden in World War II</a> towards the end of the war killed approximately 25,000 people, many of whom were civilians, and destroyed the entire city centre. After the war restoration work has helped to reconstruct parts of the historic inner city, including the <a href="/wiki/Katholische_Hofkirche" class="mw-redirect" title="Katholische Hofkirche">Katholische Hofkirche</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Zwinger_(Dresden)" title="Zwinger (Dresden)">Zwinger</a> and the famous <a href="/wiki/Semper_Oper" class="mw-redirect" title="Semper Oper">Semper Oper</a>.</p>
<p>Since <a href="/wiki/German_reunification" title="German reunification">German reunification</a> in 1990 Dresden is again a cultural, educational and political centre of Germany and Europe. The <a href="/wiki/Dresden_University_of_Technology" class="mw-redirect" title="Dresden University of Technology">Dresden University of Technology</a> is one of the 10 largest universities in Germany and part of the <a href="/wiki/German_Universities_Excellence_Initiative" title="German Universities Excellence Initiative">German Universities Excellence Initiative</a>. The economy of Dresden and its agglomeration is one of the most dynamic in Germany and ranks first in Saxony.<sup id="cite_ref-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-4">[4]</a></sup> It is dominated by <a href="/wiki/High_tech" title="High tech">high-tech branches</a>, often called as “Silicon Saxony”. The city is also one of the most visited in Germany with 4.3 million overnight stays per year.<sup id="cite_ref-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-5">[5]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-6">[6]</a></sup> The royal buildings are among the most impressive buildings in Europe. Main sights are also the nearby National Park of <a href="/wiki/Saxon_Switzerland" title="Saxon Switzerland">Saxon Switzerland</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Ore_Mountains" title="Ore Mountains">Ore Mountains</a> and the countryside around <a href="/wiki/Elbe_Valley" title="Elbe Valley">Elbe Valley</a> and <a href="/wiki/Moritzburg_Castle" title="Moritzburg Castle">Moritzburg Castle</a>. The most prominent building in the city of Dresden is the <a href="/wiki/Dresden_Frauenkirche" title="Dresden Frauenkirche">Frauenkirche</a>. Built in the 18th century, the church was destroyed during World War II. The remaining ruins were left for 50 years as a war memorial. The remains were also left in the city center of Dresden because funds to rebuild the Frauenkirche were scarce. The newly built Frauenkirche has charred stones from the destroyed church adapted with new stones as a reminder of the destruction from World War II. The church was rebuilt from 1994 to 2005.</p>
<p>According to the Hamburgische Weltwirtschaftsinstitut (HWWI) and <a href="/wiki/Berenberg_Bank" title="Berenberg Bank">Berenberg Bank</a> in 2017, Dresden has the fourth best prospects for the future of all cities in Germany.<sup id="cite_ref-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-7">[7]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-8">[8]</a></sup></p>
<p></p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="History">History</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Dresden&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: History">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">See also: <a href="/wiki/Timeline_of_Dresden" title="Timeline of Dresden">Timeline of Dresden</a></div>
<p>Although Dresden is a relatively recent city of Germanic origin followed by settlement of <a href="/wiki/Slavic_people" class="mw-redirect" title="Slavic people">Slavic people</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-Dresden.de_9-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Dresden.de-9">[9]</a></sup> the area had been settled in the <a href="/wiki/Neolithic" title="Neolithic">Neolithic</a> era by <a href="/wiki/Linear_Pottery_culture" title="Linear Pottery culture">Linear Pottery culture</a> tribes ca. <a href="/wiki/8th_millennium_BC" title="8th millennium BC">7500 BC</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-10" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-10">[10]</a></sup> Dresden's founding and early growth is associated with the <a href="/wiki/Ostsiedlung" title="Ostsiedlung">eastward expansion of Germanic peoples</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-Dresden.de_9-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Dresden.de-9">[9]</a></sup> mining in the nearby <a href="/wiki/Ore_Mountains_(Germany)" class="mw-redirect" title="Ore Mountains (Germany)">Ore Mountains</a>, and the establishment of the <a href="/wiki/Margraviate_of_Meissen" class="mw-redirect" title="Margraviate of Meissen">Margraviate of Meissen</a>. Its name etymologically derives from Old Sorbian <i>Drežďany</i>, meaning <i>people of the forest</i>. Dresden later evolved into the capital of <a href="/wiki/Saxony" title="Saxony">Saxony</a>.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Early_history">Early history</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Dresden&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Early history">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<table class="plainlinks metadata ambox mbox-small-left ambox-content" role="presentation">
<tr>
<td class="mbox-image"><a href="/wiki/File:Wiki_letter_w_cropped.svg" class="image"><img alt="[icon]" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1c/Wiki_letter_w_cropped.svg/20px-Wiki_letter_w_cropped.svg.png" width="20" height="14" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1c/Wiki_letter_w_cropped.svg/30px-Wiki_letter_w_cropped.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1c/Wiki_letter_w_cropped.svg/40px-Wiki_letter_w_cropped.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="44" data-file-height="31" /></a></td>
<td class="mbox-text">
<div class="mbox-text-span"><b>This section needs expansion</b> with: the help of German wikipedia article. <small>You can help by <a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Dresden&amp;action=edit&amp;section=">adding to it</a>.</small> <small><i>(March 2015)</i></small></div>
</td>
</tr>
</table>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Cort%C3%A8ge_des_Princes.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/eb/Cort%C3%A8ge_des_Princes.jpg/220px-Cort%C3%A8ge_des_Princes.jpg" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/eb/Cort%C3%A8ge_des_Princes.jpg/330px-Cort%C3%A8ge_des_Princes.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/eb/Cort%C3%A8ge_des_Princes.jpg/440px-Cort%C3%A8ge_des_Princes.jpg 2x" data-file-width="4000" data-file-height="3000" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Cort%C3%A8ge_des_Princes.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
The <a href="/wiki/F%C3%BCrstenzug" title="Fürstenzug">Fürstenzug</a>—the Saxon sovereigns depicted in <a href="/wiki/Meissen_porcelain" title="Meissen porcelain">Meissen porcelain</a></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Around the late 12th century, a <a href="/wiki/Slavic_peoples" class="mw-redirect" title="Slavic peoples">Slavic</a> settlement called <i>Drežďany</i><sup id="cite_ref-L.C3.B6ffler_11-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-L.C3.B6ffler-11">[11]</a></sup> had developed on the southern bank. Another settlement existed on the northern bank, but its Slavic name is unknown. It was known as <i>Antiqua Dresdin</i> by 1350, and later as Altendresden,<sup id="cite_ref-L.C3.B6ffler_11-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-L.C3.B6ffler-11">[11]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-12" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-12">[12]</a></sup> both literally "old Dresden". <a href="/wiki/Dietrich,_Margrave_of_Meissen" class="mw-redirect" title="Dietrich, Margrave of Meissen">Dietrich, Margrave of Meissen</a>, chose Dresden as his interim residence in 1206, as documented in a record calling the place "Civitas Dresdene".</p>
<p>After 1270, Dresden became the capital of the margraviate. It was given to Friedrich Clem after death of <a href="/wiki/Henry_III,_Margrave_of_Meissen" title="Henry III, Margrave of Meissen">Henry the Illustrious</a> in 1288. It was taken by the <a href="/wiki/Margraviate_of_Brandenburg" title="Margraviate of Brandenburg">Margraviate of Brandenburg</a> in 1316 and was restored to the <a href="/wiki/House_of_Wettin" title="House of Wettin">Wettin</a> dynasty after the death of <a href="/wiki/Waldemar,_Margrave_of_Brandenburg-Stendal" title="Waldemar, Margrave of Brandenburg-Stendal">Valdemar the Great</a> in 1319. From 1485, it was the seat of the dukes of <a href="/wiki/Duchy_of_Saxony" title="Duchy of Saxony">Saxony</a>, and from 1547 the <a href="/wiki/Prince-elector" title="Prince-elector">electors</a> as well.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Early-modern_age">Early-modern age</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Dresden&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Early-modern age">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:232px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Dresden_1521.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d9/Dresden_1521.jpg/230px-Dresden_1521.jpg" width="230" height="130" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d9/Dresden_1521.jpg/345px-Dresden_1521.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d9/Dresden_1521.jpg/460px-Dresden_1521.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1184" data-file-height="670" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Dresden_1521.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Dresden in 1521</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Rulers_of_Saxony" class="mw-redirect" title="Rulers of Saxony">Elector</a> and ruler of Saxony Frederick Augustus I became King <a href="/wiki/Augustus_II_the_Strong" title="Augustus II the Strong">Augustus II the Strong</a> of <a href="/wiki/Polish%E2%80%93Lithuanian_Commonwealth" title="Polish–Lithuanian Commonwealth">Poland</a> in 1697. He gathered many of the best musicians,<sup id="cite_ref-13" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-13">[13]</a></sup> architects and painters from all over Europe to the newly named Royal-Polish Residential City of Dresden.<sup id="cite_ref-14" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-14">[14]</a></sup> His reign marked the beginning of Dresden's emergence as a leading European city for technology and art. During the reign of Kings Augustus II the Strong and <a href="/wiki/Augustus_III_of_Poland" title="Augustus III of Poland">Augustus III of Poland</a> the <a href="/wiki/Zwinger_(Dresden)" title="Zwinger (Dresden)">Zwinger Royal Palace</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Dresden_Cathedral" title="Dresden Cathedral">Hofkirche</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Dresden_Frauenkirche" title="Dresden Frauenkirche">Frauenkirche</a> were built. In 1729, by decree of King Augustus II the first Polish Military Academy was founded in Dresden. In 1730, it was relocated to <a href="/wiki/Warsaw" title="Warsaw">Warsaw</a>. Dresden suffered heavy destruction in the <a href="/wiki/Seven_Years%27_War" title="Seven Years' War">Seven Years' War</a> (1756–1763), following its capture by Prussian forces, its subsequent re-capture, and a failed <a href="/wiki/Siege_of_Dresden" title="Siege of Dresden">Prussian siege</a> in 1760. <a href="/wiki/Friedrich_Schiller" title="Friedrich Schiller">Friedrich Schiller</a> wrote his "<a href="/wiki/Ode_to_Joy" title="Ode to Joy">Ode to Joy</a>" (the literary base of the <a href="/wiki/European_anthem" class="mw-redirect" title="European anthem">European anthem</a>) for the Dresden <a href="/wiki/Masonic_lodge" title="Masonic lodge">Masonic lodge</a> in 1785.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (November 2011)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup></p>
<p>The city of Dresden had a distinctive silhouette, captured in famous paintings by <a href="/wiki/Bernardo_Bellotto" title="Bernardo Bellotto">Bernardo Bellotto</a> and by Norwegian painter <a href="/wiki/Johan_Christian_Dahl" title="Johan Christian Dahl">Johan Christian Dahl</a>. Between 1806 and 1918 the city was the capital of the <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Saxony" title="Kingdom of Saxony">Kingdom of Saxony</a> (which was a part of the <a href="/wiki/German_Empire" title="German Empire">German Empire</a> from 1871). During the <a href="/wiki/Napoleonic_Wars" title="Napoleonic Wars">Napoleonic Wars</a> the <a href="/wiki/Napoleon" title="Napoleon">French emperor</a> made it a <a href="/wiki/Headquarters" title="Headquarters">base of operations</a>, winning there the famous <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Dresden" title="Battle of Dresden">Battle of Dresden</a> on 27 August 1813. Dresden was a centre of the <a href="/wiki/German_revolutions_of_1848%E2%80%9349" title="German revolutions of 1848–49">German Revolutions</a> in 1848 with the <a href="/wiki/May_Uprising_in_Dresden" title="May Uprising in Dresden">May Uprising</a>, which cost human lives and damaged the historic town of Dresden.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (November 2011)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup></p>
<p>During the 19th century, the city became a major centre of economy, including <a href="/wiki/Motor_car" class="mw-redirect" title="Motor car">motor car</a> production, food processing, banking and the manufacture of <a href="/wiki/Medical_equipment" title="Medical equipment">medical equipment</a>.</p>
<p>In the early 20th century, Dresden was particularly well known for its camera works and its cigarette factories. Between 1918 and 1934, Dresden was capital of the first Free State of Saxony. Dresden was a centre of European <a href="/wiki/Modern_art" title="Modern art">modern art</a> until 1933.</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Military_history">Military history</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Dresden&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Military history">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Dresden_photochrom2.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ae/Dresden_photochrom2.jpg/220px-Dresden_photochrom2.jpg" width="220" height="161" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ae/Dresden_photochrom2.jpg/330px-Dresden_photochrom2.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ae/Dresden_photochrom2.jpg/440px-Dresden_photochrom2.jpg 2x" data-file-width="3496" data-file-height="2561" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Dresden_photochrom2.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Image of Dresden during the 1890s, before extensive World War II destruction. Landmarks include <a href="/wiki/Dresden_Frauenkirche" title="Dresden Frauenkirche">Dresden Frauenkirche</a>, <a href="/wiki/Augustus_Bridge" title="Augustus Bridge">Augustus Bridge</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Katholische_Hofkirche" class="mw-redirect" title="Katholische Hofkirche">Katholische Hofkirche</a>.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>During the foundation of the German Empire in 1871, a large military facility called Albertstadt was built.<sup id="cite_ref-15" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-15">[15]</a></sup> It had a capacity of up to 20,000 <a href="/wiki/Soldier" title="Soldier">military personnel</a> at the beginning of the <a href="/wiki/First_World_War" class="mw-redirect" title="First World War">First World War</a>. The garrison saw only limited use between 1918 and 1934, but was then reactivated in preparation for the <a href="/wiki/Second_World_War" class="mw-redirect" title="Second World War">Second World War</a>.</p>
<p>Its usefulness was limited by attacks on 17 April 1945<sup id="cite_ref-USAFHSO_Analysis_16-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-USAFHSO_Analysis-16">[16]</a></sup> on the railway network (especially towards Bohemia).<sup id="cite_ref-17" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-17">[17]</a></sup> Soldiers had been deployed as late as March 1945 in the Albertstadt garrison.</p>
<p>The Albertstadt garrison became the headquarters of the <a href="/wiki/Soviet_1st_Guards_Tank_Army" class="mw-redirect" title="Soviet 1st Guards Tank Army">Soviet 1st Guards Tank Army</a> in the <a href="/wiki/Group_of_Soviet_Forces_in_Germany" title="Group of Soviet Forces in Germany">Group of Soviet Forces in Germany</a> after the war. Apart from the <a href="/wiki/German_Army" title="German Army">German army</a> officers' school (<i>Offizierschule des Heeres</i>), there have been no more <a href="/wiki/Military_unit" class="mw-redirect" title="Military unit">military units</a> in Dresden since the army merger during German reunification, and the withdrawal of Soviet forces in 1992. Nowadays, the Bundeswehr operates the <a href="/wiki/Milit%C3%A4rhistorisches_Museum_der_Bundeswehr" class="mw-redirect" title="Militärhistorisches Museum der Bundeswehr">Military History Museum</a> of the Federal Republic of Germany in the former Albertstadt garrison.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Second_World_War">Second World War</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Dresden&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Second World War">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Fotothek_df_ps_0000010_Blick_vom_Rathausturm.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/66/Fotothek_df_ps_0000010_Blick_vom_Rathausturm.jpg/220px-Fotothek_df_ps_0000010_Blick_vom_Rathausturm.jpg" width="220" height="220" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/66/Fotothek_df_ps_0000010_Blick_vom_Rathausturm.jpg/330px-Fotothek_df_ps_0000010_Blick_vom_Rathausturm.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/66/Fotothek_df_ps_0000010_Blick_vom_Rathausturm.jpg/440px-Fotothek_df_ps_0000010_Blick_vom_Rathausturm.jpg 2x" data-file-width="800" data-file-height="800" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Fotothek_df_ps_0000010_Blick_vom_Rathausturm.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Dresden, 1945, view from the town hall (Rathaus) over the destroyed city (the allegory of goodness in the foreground)</div>
</div>
</div>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Bombing_of_Dresden_in_World_War_II" title="Bombing of Dresden in World War II">Bombing of Dresden in World War II</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Bundesarchiv_Bild_183-Z0309-310,_Zerst%C3%B6rtes_Dresden.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/96/Bundesarchiv_Bild_183-Z0309-310%2C_Zerst%C3%B6rtes_Dresden.jpg/220px-Bundesarchiv_Bild_183-Z0309-310%2C_Zerst%C3%B6rtes_Dresden.jpg" width="220" height="149" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/96/Bundesarchiv_Bild_183-Z0309-310%2C_Zerst%C3%B6rtes_Dresden.jpg/330px-Bundesarchiv_Bild_183-Z0309-310%2C_Zerst%C3%B6rtes_Dresden.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/96/Bundesarchiv_Bild_183-Z0309-310%2C_Zerst%C3%B6rtes_Dresden.jpg/440px-Bundesarchiv_Bild_183-Z0309-310%2C_Zerst%C3%B6rtes_Dresden.jpg 2x" data-file-width="795" data-file-height="537" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Bundesarchiv_Bild_183-Z0309-310,_Zerst%C3%B6rtes_Dresden.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Dresden, 1945—over 90 percent of the city centre was destroyed.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>During the Nazi era from 1933 to 1945, the Jewish community of Dresden was reduced from over 6,000 (7,100 people were persecuted as Jews) to 41.<sup id="cite_ref-18" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-18">[18]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-19" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-19">[19]</a></sup> Non-Jews were also targeted, and over 1,300 people were executed by the Nazis at the Münchner Platz, a courthouse in Dresden, including labour leaders, undesirables, resistance fighters and anyone caught listening to foreign radio broadcasts.<sup id="cite_ref-20" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-20">[20]</a></sup> The bombing stopped prisoners who were busy digging a large hole into which an additional 4,000 prisoners were to be disposed of.<sup id="cite_ref-21" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-21">[21]</a></sup></p>
<p>Dresden in the 20th century was a major communications hub and manufacturing centre with 127 factories and major workshops and was designated by the German Military as a defensive strongpoint, with which to hinder the Soviet advance.<sup id="cite_ref-mirror_22-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-mirror-22">[22]</a></sup> Being the capital of the German state of <a href="/wiki/Saxony" title="Saxony">Saxony</a>, Dresden not only had garrisons but a whole <i>military borough</i>, the <i>Albertstadt</i>.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (March 2015)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> This military complex, named after <a href="/wiki/Saxon" class="mw-redirect" title="Saxon">Saxon</a> King Albert, was not specifically targeted in the <a href="/wiki/Bombing_of_Dresden_in_World_War_II" title="Bombing of Dresden in World War II">bombing of Dresden</a>, though it was within the expected area of destruction and was extensively damaged.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (March 2015)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup></p>
<p>During the final months of the Second World War, Dresden harboured some 600,000 refugees, with a total population of <span class="nowrap">1.2 million</span>. Dresden was attacked seven times between 1944 and 1945, and was occupied by the <a href="/wiki/Red_Army" title="Red Army">Red Army</a> after the German capitulation.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (March 2015)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup></p>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Bombing_of_Dresden_in_World_War_II" title="Bombing of Dresden in World War II">bombing of Dresden</a> by the <a href="/wiki/Royal_Air_Force" title="Royal Air Force">Royal Air Force</a> (RAF) and the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Army_Air_Forces" title="United States Army Air Forces">United States Army Air Forces</a> (USAAF) between 13 and 15 February 1945 remains controversial.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (March 2015)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> The inner city of Dresden was largely destroyed<sup id="cite_ref-23" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-23">[23]</a></sup> by 722 RAF and 527 USAAF bombers that dropped 2431 tons of high explosive bombs, and 1475.9 tons of incendiaries.<sup id="cite_ref-24" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-24">[24]</a></sup> The high explosive bombs damaged buildings and exposed their wooden structures, while the incendiaries ignited them, denying their use by retreating German troops and refugees.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (March 2015)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> Widely quoted Nazi propaganda reports claimed 200,000 deaths however the German <a href="/w/index.php?title=Dresden_Historians%27_Commission&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Dresden Historians' Commission (page does not exist)">Dresden Historians' Commission</a> made up of 13 prominent German historians, in an official 2010 report published after five years of research concluded that casualties numbered between 18,000 and a maximum of 25,000.<sup id="cite_ref-25" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-25">[25]</a></sup> The Allies described the operation as the legitimate bombing of a military and industrial target.<sup id="cite_ref-USAFHSO_Analysis_16-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-USAFHSO_Analysis-16">[16]</a></sup> Several researchers have argued that the February attacks were <a href="/wiki/Proportionality_(law)#International_law" title="Proportionality (law)">disproportionate</a>. However, mostly women and children died.<sup id="cite_ref-26" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-26">[26]</a></sup> When interviewed after the war in 1977, <a href="/wiki/Sir_Arthur_Harris,_1st_Baronet" title="Sir Arthur Harris, 1st Baronet">Sir Arthur Harris</a> stood by his decision to carry out the raids, and reaffirmed that it reduced the German military's ability to wage war.<sup id="cite_ref-27" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-27">[27]</a></sup></p>
<p>American author <a href="/wiki/Kurt_Vonnegut" title="Kurt Vonnegut">Kurt Vonnegut</a>'s novel <i><a href="/wiki/Slaughterhouse_Five" class="mw-redirect" title="Slaughterhouse Five">Slaughterhouse Five</a></i> is loosely based on his first-hand experience of the raid as a <a href="/wiki/POW" class="mw-redirect" title="POW">POW</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-28" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-28">[28]</a></sup> In remembrance of the victims, the anniversaries of the bombing of Dresden are marked with peace demonstrations, devotions and marches.<sup id="cite_ref-29" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-29">[29]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-30" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-30">[30]</a></sup></p>
<p>The destruction of Dresden allowed <a href="/wiki/Hildebrand_Gurlitt" title="Hildebrand Gurlitt">Hildebrand Gurlitt</a>, a major Nazi museum director and art dealer, to hide a large collection of artwork worth over a billion dollars that had been stolen during the Nazi era, as he claimed it had been destroyed along with his house which was located in Dresden.<sup id="cite_ref-31" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-31">[31]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Post-war_period">Post-war period</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Dresden&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Post-war period">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>After the Second World War, Dresden became a major industrial centre in the <a href="/wiki/German_Democratic_Republic" class="mw-redirect" title="German Democratic Republic">German Democratic Republic</a> (former East Germany) with a great deal of research infrastructure. It was the centre of <a href="/wiki/Bezirk_Dresden" title="Bezirk Dresden">Bezirk Dresden</a> (Dresden District) between 1952 and 1990. Many of the city's important historic buildings were reconstructed, including the <a href="/wiki/Semper_Opera_House" class="mw-redirect" title="Semper Opera House">Semper Opera House</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Zwinger_Palace" class="mw-redirect" title="Zwinger Palace">Zwinger Palace</a>, although the city leaders chose to rebuild large areas of the city in a "socialist modern" style, partly for economic reasons, but also to break away from the city's past as the royal capital of Saxony and a stronghold of the German bourgeoisie. Some of the ruins of churches, royal buildings and palaces, such as the Gothic <a href="/wiki/Sophienkirche" title="Sophienkirche">Sophienkirche</a>, the <a href="/w/index.php?title=Alberttheater&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Alberttheater (page does not exist)">Alberttheater</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Wackerbarth-Palais" title="Wackerbarth-Palais">Wackerbarth-Palais</a>, were razed by the Soviet and East German authorities in the 1950s and 1960s rather than being repaired. Compared to <a href="/wiki/West_Germany" title="West Germany">West Germany</a>, the majority of historic buildings were saved.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (December 2012)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup></p>
<p>From 1985 to 1990, the future <a href="/wiki/President_of_the_Russian_Federation" class="mw-redirect" title="President of the Russian Federation">President of Russia</a>, <a href="/wiki/Vladimir_Putin" title="Vladimir Putin">Vladimir Putin</a>, was stationed in Dresden by the <a href="/wiki/KGB" title="KGB">KGB</a>, where he worked for <a href="/wiki/Lazar_Matveev" title="Lazar Matveev">Lazar Matveev</a>, the senior <a href="/wiki/KGB" title="KGB">KGB</a> liaison officer there. On 3 October 1989 (the so-called "battle of Dresden"), a convoy of trains carrying East German refugees from <a href="/wiki/Prague" title="Prague">Prague</a> passed through Dresden on its way to the <a href="/wiki/West_Germany" title="West Germany">Federal Republic of Germany</a>. Local activists and residents joined in the growing <a href="/wiki/Civil_disobedience" title="Civil disobedience">civil disobedience</a> movement spreading across the German Democratic Republic, by staging demonstrations and demanding the removal of the non-democratic government.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Post-reunification">Post-reunification</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Dresden&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Post-reunification">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Sonnedd.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b7/Sonnedd.jpg/220px-Sonnedd.jpg" width="220" height="163" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b7/Sonnedd.jpg/330px-Sonnedd.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/b/b7/Sonnedd.jpg 2x" data-file-width="400" data-file-height="296" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Sonnedd.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
The <a href="/wiki/Dresden_Frauenkirche" title="Dresden Frauenkirche">Dresden Frauenkirche</a>, a few years after its reconsecration</div>
</div>
</div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:DD_Altstadt_Semperoper.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2b/DD_Altstadt_Semperoper.jpg/220px-DD_Altstadt_Semperoper.jpg" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2b/DD_Altstadt_Semperoper.jpg/330px-DD_Altstadt_Semperoper.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2b/DD_Altstadt_Semperoper.jpg/440px-DD_Altstadt_Semperoper.jpg 2x" data-file-width="4000" data-file-height="3000" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:DD_Altstadt_Semperoper.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Dresden old town</div>
</div>
</div>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Dresden_Frauenkirche_Nacht.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2f/Dresden_Frauenkirche_Nacht.jpg/220px-Dresden_Frauenkirche_Nacht.jpg" width="220" height="147" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2f/Dresden_Frauenkirche_Nacht.jpg/330px-Dresden_Frauenkirche_Nacht.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2f/Dresden_Frauenkirche_Nacht.jpg/440px-Dresden_Frauenkirche_Nacht.jpg 2x" data-file-width="5477" data-file-height="3651" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Dresden_Frauenkirche_Nacht.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Dresden Frauenkirche at night</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Dresden has experienced dramatic changes since the reunification of Germany in the early 1990s. The city still bears many wounds from the <a href="/wiki/Strategic_bombing" title="Strategic bombing">bombing raids</a> of 1945, but it has undergone significant reconstruction in recent decades. Restoration of the <a href="/wiki/Dresden_Frauenkirche" title="Dresden Frauenkirche">Dresden Frauenkirche</a> was completed in 2005, a year before Dresden's 800th anniversary, notably by privately raised funds. The gold cross on the top of the church was funded officially by "the British people and the House of Windsor". The <a href="/wiki/Urban_renewal" title="Urban renewal">urban renewal</a> process, which includes the reconstruction of the area around the <a href="/wiki/Neumarkt_(Dresden)" title="Neumarkt (Dresden)">Neumarkt square</a> on which the Frauenkirche is situated, will continue for many decades, but public and government interest remains high, and there are numerous large projects underway—both historic reconstructions and modern plans—that will continue the city's recent architectural renaissance.</p>
<p>Dresden remains a major cultural centre of historical memory, owing to the city's destruction in World War II. Each year on 13 February, the anniversary of the <a href="/wiki/Bombing_of_Dresden_in_World_War_II" title="Bombing of Dresden in World War II">British and American fire-bombing raid</a> that destroyed most of the city, tens of thousands of demonstrators gather to commemorate the event. Since reunification, the ceremony has taken on a more neutral and pacifist tone (after being used more politically during the <a href="/wiki/Cold_War" title="Cold War">Cold War</a>). Beginning in 1999, <a href="/wiki/White_Nationalism" class="mw-redirect" title="White Nationalism">white nationalists</a> have organised <a href="/wiki/Neo-Nazi" class="mw-redirect" title="Neo-Nazi">Neo-Nazi</a> demonstrations in Dresden that have been among the largest in the post-war <a href="/wiki/History_of_Germany" title="History of Germany">history of Germany</a>. Each year around the anniversary of the city's destruction, members of the <a href="/wiki/Far-right_politics_in_Germany" title="Far-right politics in Germany">far-right</a> convened in the memory of those who died in the fire-bombing.</p>
<p>The completion of the reconstructed Dresden Frauenkirche in 2005 marked the first step in rebuilding the <a href="/wiki/Dresden_Neumarkt" class="mw-redirect" title="Dresden Neumarkt">Neumarkt</a> area. The areas around the square have been divided into 8 "Quarters", with each being rebuilt as a separate project, the majority of buildings to be rebuilt either to the original structure or at least with a façade similar to the original. Quarter I and the front section of Quarters II, III, IV and V(II) have since been completed, with Quarter VIII currently under construction.</p>
<p>In 2002, torrential rains caused the <a href="/wiki/Elbe" title="Elbe">Elbe</a> to flood 9 metres (30&#160;ft) above its normal height, i.e., even higher than the old record height from 1845, damaging many landmarks (See <a href="/wiki/2002_European_flood" class="mw-redirect" title="2002 European flood">2002 European flood</a>). The destruction from this "millennium flood" is no longer visible, due to the speed of reconstruction.</p>
<p>The United Nations' cultural organization <a href="/wiki/UNESCO" title="UNESCO">UNESCO</a> declared the <a href="/wiki/Dresden_Elbe_Valley" title="Dresden Elbe Valley">Dresden Elbe Valley</a> to be a World Heritage Site in 2004.<sup id="cite_ref-32" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-32">[32]</a></sup> After being placed on the list of endangered World Heritage Sites in 2006, the city lost the title in June 2009,<sup id="cite_ref-DW_2009-06-25_33-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-DW_2009-06-25-33">[33]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Guardian_2009-06-25_34-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Guardian_2009-06-25-34">[34]</a></sup> due to the construction of the <i><a href="/wiki/Waldschl%C3%B6%C3%9Fchenbr%C3%BCcke" class="mw-redirect" title="Waldschlößchenbrücke">Waldschlößchenbrücke</a></i>, making it only the second ever World Heritage Site to be removed from the register.<sup id="cite_ref-DW_2009-06-25_33-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-DW_2009-06-25-33">[33]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Guardian_2009-06-25_34-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Guardian_2009-06-25-34">[34]</a></sup> UNESCO stated in 2006 that the bridge would destroy the cultural landscape. The city council's legal moves, meant to prevent the bridge from being built, failed.<sup id="cite_ref-35" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-35">[35]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-36" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-36">[36]</a></sup></p>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Dresden_Elbe_Valley" title="Dresden Elbe Valley">Dresden Elbe Valley</a> was an internationally recognised site of cultural significance by the <a href="/wiki/UNESCO" title="UNESCO">UNESCO</a> <a href="/wiki/World_Heritage_Committee" title="World Heritage Committee">World Heritage Committee</a> for five years. After being placed on the <a href="/wiki/List_of_World_Heritage_in_Danger" title="List of World Heritage in Danger">list of endangered World Heritage Sites</a> in 2006, the city had its status as world heritage site formally removed in June 2009, for the wilful breach of the UNESCO World Heritage Convention, due to the construction of a highway bridge across the valley within 2&#160;km (1&#160;mi) of the historic centre. The "Free State of Saxony" is responsible for the destruction of this unique cultural landscape. It thereby became the first location ever in Europe to lose this status, and the second ever in the world.<sup id="cite_ref-37" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-37">[37]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tnone" style="margin-left: auto; margin-right:auto; overflow:hidden; width:auto; max-width:1008px;">

Modern Dresden by night</div>
</div>
</div>
<div class="thumb tnone" style="margin-left: auto; margin-right:auto; overflow:hidden; width:auto; max-width:1008px;">

</div>
</div>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Geography">Geography</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Dresden&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Geography">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Geography_and_urban_development_of_Dresden" title="Geography and urban development of Dresden">Geography and urban development of Dresden</a></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Location">Location</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Dresden&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Location">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Basteibr%C3%BCcke_morgens_(Zuschnitt).jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4a/Basteibr%C3%BCcke_morgens_%28Zuschnitt%29.jpg/220px-Basteibr%C3%BCcke_morgens_%28Zuschnitt%29.jpg" width="220" height="157" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4a/Basteibr%C3%BCcke_morgens_%28Zuschnitt%29.jpg/330px-Basteibr%C3%BCcke_morgens_%28Zuschnitt%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4a/Basteibr%C3%BCcke_morgens_%28Zuschnitt%29.jpg/440px-Basteibr%C3%BCcke_morgens_%28Zuschnitt%29.jpg 2x" data-file-width="4825" data-file-height="3452" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Basteibr%C3%BCcke_morgens_(Zuschnitt).jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/Saxon_Switzerland" title="Saxon Switzerland">Saxon Switzerland</a> a few kilometres outside of Dresden</div>
</div>
</div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Pillnitz_k%C3%B6niglicher_Weinberg_(04-2).jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ec/Pillnitz_k%C3%B6niglicher_Weinberg_%2804-2%29.jpg/220px-Pillnitz_k%C3%B6niglicher_Weinberg_%2804-2%29.jpg" width="220" height="83" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ec/Pillnitz_k%C3%B6niglicher_Weinberg_%2804-2%29.jpg/330px-Pillnitz_k%C3%B6niglicher_Weinberg_%2804-2%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ec/Pillnitz_k%C3%B6niglicher_Weinberg_%2804-2%29.jpg/440px-Pillnitz_k%C3%B6niglicher_Weinberg_%2804-2%29.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1600" data-file-height="607" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Pillnitz_k%C3%B6niglicher_Weinberg_(04-2).jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
View over <a href="/wiki/Dresden_Basin" title="Dresden Basin">Dresden Basin</a></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Dresden lies on both banks of the <a href="/wiki/Elbe_River" class="mw-redirect" title="Elbe River">Elbe River</a>, mostly in the <a href="/wiki/Dresden_Basin" title="Dresden Basin">Dresden Basin</a>, with the further reaches of the eastern <a href="/wiki/Ore_Mountains_(Germany)" class="mw-redirect" title="Ore Mountains (Germany)">Ore Mountains</a> to the south, the steep slope of the <a href="/wiki/Lusatia" title="Lusatia">Lusatian</a> granitic crust to the north, and the <a href="/wiki/Elbe_Sandstone_Mountains" title="Elbe Sandstone Mountains">Elbe Sandstone Mountains</a> to the east at an altitude of about 113 metres (371 feet). Triebenberg is the highest point in Dresden at 384 metres (1,260 feet).<sup id="cite_ref-basicGeoData_38-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-basicGeoData-38">[38]</a></sup></p>
<p>With a pleasant location and a mild climate on the Elbe, as well as Baroque-style architecture and numerous world-renowned museums and art collections, Dresden has been called "Elbflorenz" (<a href="/wiki/Florence" title="Florence">Florence</a> of the Elbe). The incorporation of neighbouring <a href="/wiki/Types_of_rural_communities" title="Types of rural communities">rural communities</a> over the past 60 years has made Dresden the fourth largest <a href="/wiki/Urban_districts_of_Germany" class="mw-redirect" title="Urban districts of Germany">urban district</a> by area in Germany after Berlin, Hamburg and <a href="/wiki/Cologne" title="Cologne">Cologne</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-cityList_39-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-cityList-39">[39]</a></sup></p>
<p>The nearest German cities are <a href="/wiki/Chemnitz" title="Chemnitz">Chemnitz</a> 80 kilometres (50 miles) to the southwest, <a href="/wiki/Leipzig" title="Leipzig">Leipzig</a> 100 kilometres (62 miles) to the northwest and Berlin 200 kilometres (120 miles) to the north. <a href="/wiki/Prague" title="Prague">Prague</a>, <a href="/wiki/Czech_Republic" title="Czech Republic">Czech Republic</a> is about 150 kilometres (93 miles) to the south and to the east 200 kilometres (120 miles) is the <a href="/wiki/List_of_cities_in_Poland" class="mw-redirect" title="List of cities in Poland">Polish city</a> of <a href="/wiki/Wroc%C5%82aw" title="Wrocław">Wrocław</a>.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Nature">Nature</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Dresden&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Nature">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Dresden is one of the greenest cities in all of Europe, with 63% of the city being green areas and forests. The <a href="/wiki/Dresden_Heath" title="Dresden Heath">Dresden Heath</a> (<i>Dresdner Heide</i>) to the north is a forest 50&#160;km<sup>2</sup> in size. There are four <a href="/wiki/Nature_reserve" title="Nature reserve">nature reserves</a>. The additional Special Conservation Areas cover 18&#160;km<sup>2</sup>. The protected gardens, parkways, parks and old graveyards host 110 natural monuments in the city.<sup id="cite_ref-40" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-40">[40]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Dresden_Elbe_Valley" title="Dresden Elbe Valley">Dresden Elbe Valley</a> is a former world heritage site which is focused on the conservation of the <a href="/wiki/Cultural_landscape" title="Cultural landscape">cultural landscape</a> in Dresden. One important part of that landscape is the Elbe meadows, which cross the city in a 20 kilometre swath. <a href="/wiki/Saxon_Switzerland" title="Saxon Switzerland">Saxon Switzerland</a> is an important nearby location.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Climate">Climate</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Dresden&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Climate">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Dresden has an <a href="/wiki/Oceanic_climate" title="Oceanic climate">oceanic climate</a> (<a href="/wiki/Oceanic_climate" title="Oceanic climate">Cfb</a>), influenced by its inland location, with warm summers and slightly colder winters as compared to the German average. The average temperature in January is 0.1&#160;°C (32.18&#160;°F) and in July 19.0&#160;°C (66.2&#160;°F). The driest months are February, March and April, with precipitation of around 40&#160;mm (1.6&#160;in). The wettest months are July and August, with more than 80&#160;mm (3.1&#160;in) per month.</p>
<p>The microclimate in the <a href="/wiki/Elbe_valley" class="mw-redirect" title="Elbe valley">Elbe valley</a> differs from that on the slopes and in the higher areas, where the Dresden district <a href="/wiki/Klotzsche" title="Klotzsche">Klotzsche</a>, at 227 metres <a href="/wiki/Above_mean_sea_level" class="mw-redirect" title="Above mean sea level">above sea level</a>, hosts the Dresden <a href="/wiki/Weather_station" title="Weather station">weather station</a>. The weather in Klotzsche is 1 to 3&#160;°C (1.8 to 5.4&#160;°F) colder than in the <a href="/wiki/Inner_city" title="Inner city">inner city</a> at 112 metres <a href="/wiki/Above_mean_sea_level" class="mw-redirect" title="Above mean sea level">above sea level</a>.</p>

<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Flood_protection">Flood protection</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Dresden&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Flood protection">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Because of its location on the banks of the Elbe, into which some water sources from the Ore Mountains flow, flood protection is important. Large areas are kept free of buildings to provide a flood plain. Two additional trenches, about 50 metres wide, have been built to keep the inner city free of water from the Elbe, by dissipating the water downstream through the inner city's gorge portion. Flood regulation systems like <a href="/wiki/Detention_basin" title="Detention basin">detention basins</a> and <a href="/wiki/Water_reservoir" class="mw-redirect" title="Water reservoir">water reservoirs</a> are almost all outside the <a href="/wiki/Urban_area" title="Urban area">city area</a>.</p>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Wei%C3%9Feritz" title="Weißeritz">Weißeritz</a>, normally a rather small river, suddenly ran directly into the main station of Dresden during the <a href="/wiki/2002_European_floods" title="2002 European floods">2002 European floods</a>. This was largely because the river returned to its former route; it had been diverted so that a railway could run along the river bed.</p>
<p>Many locations and areas need to be protected by walls and sheet pilings during floods. A number of districts become waterlogged if the Elbe overflows across some of its former floodplains.</p>

<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="City_structuring">City structuring</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Dresden&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: City structuring">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tleft">

<p>Dresden is a spacious city. Its districts differ in their structure and appearance. Many parts still contain an old village core, while some quarters are almost completely preserved as rural settings. Other characteristic kinds of urban areas are the historic outskirts of the city, and the former suburbs with scattered housing. During the German Democratic Republic, many apartment blocks were built. The original parts of the city are almost all in the districts of Altstadt (Old town) and Neustadt (New town). Growing outside the <a href="/wiki/Defensive_wall" title="Defensive wall">city walls</a>, the historic outskirts were built in the 18th century. They were planned and constructed on the orders of the Saxon monarchs, which is why the outskirts are often named after sovereigns. From the 19th century the city grew by incorporating other districts. Dresden has been divided into ten districts called "Ortsamtsbereich" and nine former boroughs ("Ortschaften") which have been incorporated.</p>

<p>The population of Dresden grew to 100,000 inhabitants in 1852, making it one of the first German cities after Hamburg and Berlin to reach that number. The population peaked at 649,252 in 1933, and dropped to 450,000 in 1946 because of World War II, during which large residential areas of the city were destroyed. After large incorporations and city restoration, the population grew to 522,532 again between 1950 and 1983.<sup id="cite_ref-demogr1_43-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-demogr1-43">[43]</a></sup></p>
<p>Since <a href="/wiki/German_reunification" title="German reunification">German reunification</a>, demographic development has been very unsteady. The city has struggled with migration and suburbanisation. During the 1990s the population increased to 480,000 because of several incorporations, and decreased to 452,827 in 1998. Between 2000 and 2010, the population grew quickly by more than 45,000 inhabitants (about 9.5%) due to a stabilised economy and re-urbanisation. Along with <a href="/wiki/Munich" title="Munich">Munich</a> and <a href="/wiki/Potsdam" title="Potsdam">Potsdam</a>, Dresden is one of the ten fastest-growing cities in Germany,<sup id="cite_ref-cityList_39-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-cityList-39">[39]</a></sup> while the population of the surrounding <a href="/wiki/New_states_of_Germany" title="New states of Germany">new federal states</a> is still shrinking.<sup id="cite_ref-demogr1_43-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-demogr1-43">[43]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-44" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-44">[44]</a></sup></p>
<p>As of 2010<sup class="plainlinks noprint asof-tag update" style="display:none;"><a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Dresden&amp;action=edit">[update]</a></sup> the population of the city of Dresden was 523,058,<sup id="cite_ref-45" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-45">[45]</a></sup> the population of the Dresden agglomeration was 780,561 as of 2008<sup class="plainlinks noprint asof-tag update" style="display:none;"><a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Dresden&amp;action=edit">[update]</a></sup>,<sup id="cite_ref-46" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-46">[46]</a></sup> and as of 2007<sup class="plainlinks noprint asof-tag update" style="display:none;"><a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Dresden&amp;action=edit">[update]</a></sup> the population of the Dresden region, which includes the neighbouring districts of <a href="/wiki/Mei%C3%9Fen_(district)" class="mw-redirect" title="Meißen (district)">Meißen</a>, <a href="/wiki/S%C3%A4chsische_Schweiz-Osterzgebirge" title="Sächsische Schweiz-Osterzgebirge">Sächsische Schweiz-Osterzgebirge</a> and the western part of the district of <a href="/wiki/Bautzen_(district)" title="Bautzen (district)">Bautzen</a> was 1,143,197.<sup id="cite_ref-47" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-47">[47]</a></sup> Dresden is one of the few German Cities which has more inhabitants than ever since World War II.</p>
<p>As of 2006<sup class="plainlinks noprint asof-tag update" style="display:none;"><a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Dresden&amp;action=edit">[update]</a></sup> about 51.3% of the population was female.<sup id="cite_ref-dre06_48-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-dre06-48">[48]</a></sup> As of 2007<sup class="plainlinks noprint asof-tag update" style="display:none;"><a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Dresden&amp;action=edit">[update]</a></sup> the mean age of the population was 43 years, which is the lowest among the urban districts in Saxony.<sup id="cite_ref-49" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-49">[49]</a></sup> As of 31 December 2013<sup class="plainlinks noprint asof-tag update" style="display:none;"><a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Dresden&amp;action=edit">[update]</a></sup> there were 43,707 people with a migration background (8.7% of the city's population), and about half, 25,224 or about 4.7% of all Dresden citizens were foreigners.<sup id="cite_ref-50" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-50">[50]</a></sup> This percentage is almost the same as in 2006 with 4%.<sup id="cite_ref-dre06_48-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-dre06-48">[48]</a></sup></p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Governance">Governance</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Dresden&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Governance">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/City_Council_of_Dresden" title="City Council of Dresden">City Council of Dresden</a></div>
<p>Dresden is one of Germany's 16 political centres and the capital of Saxony. It has institutions of democratic local self-administration that are independent from the capital functions.<sup id="cite_ref-51" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-51">[51]</a></sup> Some local affairs of Dresden receive national attention.</p>
<p>Dresden hosted some international summits such as the Petersburg Dialogue between Russia and Germany, the European Union's <a href="/wiki/Interior_minister" class="mw-redirect" title="Interior minister">Minister of the Interior</a> conference and the <a href="/wiki/G8" class="mw-redirect" title="G8">G8</a> labour ministers conference in recent years.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (January 2015)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Municipality_and_city_council">Municipality and city council</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Dresden&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Municipality and city council">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/City_council" title="City council">city council</a> defines the basic principles of the municipality by decrees and statutes. The council gives orders to the "Bürgermeister" ("Burgomaster" or Mayor) by voting for resolutions and thus has some executive power.<sup id="cite_ref-52" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-52">[52]</a></sup> As of 2008<sup class="plainlinks noprint asof-tag update" style="display:none;"><a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Dresden&amp;action=edit">[update]</a></sup>, there was no stable governing majority on Dresden city council (Stadtrat).<sup id="cite_ref-53" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-53">[53]</a></sup></p>
<p>As of 2014<sup class="plainlinks noprint asof-tag update" style="display:none;"><a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Dresden&amp;action=edit">[update]</a></sup> the 70 seats of the city council were distributed as follows:<sup id="cite_ref-dre2014_54-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-dre2014-54">[54]</a></sup></p>
<table class="wikitable">

<p>The <a href="/wiki/Burgomaster" title="Burgomaster">Supreme Burgomaster</a> is directly elected by the citizens for a term of seven years. Executive functions are normally elected indirectly in Germany. However, the Supreme Burgomaster shares numerous executive rights with the city council. He/She is the executive head of the municipality, and also the ceremonial representative of the city. The main departments of the municipality are managed by seven burgomasters.<sup id="cite_ref-55" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-55">[55]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Local_affairs">Local affairs</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Dresden&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Local affairs">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Waldschl%C3%B6%C3%9Fchenbr%C3%BCcke_2013_Dresden_004.JPG" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/45/Waldschl%C3%B6%C3%9Fchenbr%C3%BCcke_2013_Dresden_004.JPG/220px-Waldschl%C3%B6%C3%9Fchenbr%C3%BCcke_2013_Dresden_004.JPG" width="220" height="124" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/45/Waldschl%C3%B6%C3%9Fchenbr%C3%BCcke_2013_Dresden_004.JPG/330px-Waldschl%C3%B6%C3%9Fchenbr%C3%BCcke_2013_Dresden_004.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/45/Waldschl%C3%B6%C3%9Fchenbr%C3%BCcke_2013_Dresden_004.JPG/440px-Waldschl%C3%B6%C3%9Fchenbr%C3%BCcke_2013_Dresden_004.JPG 2x" data-file-width="4320" data-file-height="2432" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Waldschl%C3%B6%C3%9Fchenbr%C3%BCcke_2013_Dresden_004.JPG" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
The <a href="/wiki/Waldschl%C3%B6sschen_Bridge" title="Waldschlösschen Bridge">Waldschlösschen Bridge</a> is a subject of controversy in Dresden and other parts of Germany</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Local affairs in Dresden often centre around the <a href="/wiki/Urban_planning" title="Urban planning">urban development</a> of the city and its spaces. Architecture and the design of <a href="/wiki/Public_space" title="Public space">public places</a> is a controversial subject. Discussions about the <a href="/wiki/Waldschl%C3%B6%C3%9Fchenbr%C3%BCcke" class="mw-redirect" title="Waldschlößchenbrücke">Waldschlößchenbrücke</a>, a bridge under construction across the Elbe, received international attention because of its position across the <a href="/wiki/Dresden_Elbe_Valley" title="Dresden Elbe Valley">Dresden Elbe Valley</a> <a href="/wiki/World_Heritage_Site" title="World Heritage Site">World Heritage Site</a>. The city held a public referendum in 2005 on whether to build the bridge, prior to UNESCO expressing doubts about the compatibility between bridge and heritage. Its construction caused loss of World Heritage site status in 2009.<sup id="cite_ref-56" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-56">[56]</a></sup></p>
<p>In 2006 Dresden sold its publicly <a href="/wiki/Subsidized_housing" title="Subsidized housing">subsidized housing</a> organization, WOBA Dresden GmbH, to the US-based private <a href="/wiki/Investment_company" title="Investment company">investment company</a> <a href="/wiki/Fortress_Investment_Group" title="Fortress Investment Group">Fortress Investment Group</a>. The city received <span class="nowrap">987.1 million</span> euro and paid off its remaining loans, making it the first large city in Germany to become debt-free. Opponents of the sale were concerned about Dresden's loss of control over the <a href="/wiki/Subsidized_housing" title="Subsidized housing">subsidized housing market</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-57" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-57">[57]</a></sup></p>
<p>Since October 2014, <a href="/wiki/PEGIDA" class="mw-redirect" title="PEGIDA">PEGIDA</a>, a <a href="/wiki/Nationalistic" class="mw-redirect" title="Nationalistic">nationalistic</a> political movement based in Dresden has been organising weekly demonstrations against what it perceives as the <a href="/wiki/Islamisation" class="mw-redirect" title="Islamisation">Islamisation</a> of Europe although the primarily <a href="/wiki/Turks_in_Germany" title="Turks in Germany">Turkish</a> and <a href="/wiki/Muslims_in_Germany" class="mw-redirect" title="Muslims in Germany">Muslim population</a> make up only 0.2% of the population of the city. As the number of demonstrators increased to 17,500 on December 22, so has the international media coverage of it.<sup id="cite_ref-Kirshbaum16_58-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Kirshbaum16-58">[58]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span id="Twin_towns_–_sister_cities"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Twin_towns_.E2.80.93_sister_cities">Twin towns – sister cities</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Dresden&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Twin towns – sister cities">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">See also: <a href="/wiki/List_of_twin_towns_and_sister_cities_in_Germany" title="List of twin towns and sister cities in Germany">List of twin towns and sister cities in Germany</a></div>
<p>Along with its <a href="/wiki/Town_twinning" class="mw-redirect" title="Town twinning">twin city</a> <a href="/wiki/Coventry" title="Coventry">Coventry</a> in <a href="/wiki/England" title="England">England</a>, Dresden was one of the first two cities to pair with a foreign city after <a href="/wiki/World_War_II" title="World War II">the Second World War</a>.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (April 2015)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> The cities became twins after the war in an act of <a href="/wiki/Vergangenheitsbew%C3%A4ltigung" title="Vergangenheitsbewältigung">reconciliation</a>, as both had suffered near-total destruction from <a href="/wiki/Strategic_bombing" title="Strategic bombing">massive aerial bombing</a>.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (April 2015)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> Similar symbolism occurred in 1988, when Dresden twinned with the <a href="/wiki/Netherlands" title="Netherlands">Dutch</a> city of <a href="/wiki/Rotterdam" title="Rotterdam">Rotterdam</a>. The <a href="/wiki/Coventry_Blitz" title="Coventry Blitz">Coventry Blitz</a> and <a href="/wiki/Rotterdam_Blitz" class="mw-redirect" title="Rotterdam Blitz">Rotterdam Blitz</a> bombardments by the German <a href="/wiki/Luftwaffe" title="Luftwaffe">Luftwaffe</a> are also considered to be <a href="/wiki/Proportionality_(law)#International_law" title="Proportionality (law)">disproportional</a>.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (April 2015)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup></p>
<p>Dresden has had a triangular partnership with <a href="/wiki/Saint_Petersburg" title="Saint Petersburg">Saint Petersburg</a> and <a href="/wiki/Hamburg" title="Hamburg">Hamburg</a> since 1987. Dresden has 14 <a href="/wiki/Town_twinning" class="mw-redirect" title="Town twinning">twin cities</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Dresden_59-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Dresden-59">[59]</a></sup></p>
<ul>
<li><span class="flagicon"><a href="/wiki/England" title="England"><img alt="England" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/b/be/Flag_of_England.svg/23px-Flag_of_England.svg.png" width="23" height="14" class="thumbborder" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/b/be/Flag_of_England.svg/35px-Flag_of_England.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/b/be/Flag_of_England.svg/46px-Flag_of_England.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="800" data-file-height="480" /></a></span> <a href="/wiki/Coventry" title="Coventry">Coventry</a>, <a href="/wiki/West_Midlands_(county)" title="West Midlands (county)">West Midlands</a>, England, <a href="/wiki/United_Kingdom" title="United Kingdom">United Kingdom</a>, since 1959<sup id="cite_ref-Coventry_twinnings_60-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Coventry_twinnings-60">[60]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Coventry_twins_61-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Coventry_twins-61">[61]</a></sup></li>
<li><span class="flagicon"><a href="/wiki/Russia" title="Russia"><img alt="Russia" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/f/f3/Flag_of_Russia.svg/23px-Flag_of_Russia.svg.png" width="23" height="15" class="thumbborder" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/f/f3/Flag_of_Russia.svg/35px-Flag_of_Russia.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/f/f3/Flag_of_Russia.svg/45px-Flag_of_Russia.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="900" data-file-height="600" /></a></span> <a href="/wiki/Saint_Petersburg" title="Saint Petersburg">Saint Petersburg</a>, Russia, since 1961</li>
<li><span class="flagicon"><a href="/wiki/Poland" title="Poland"><img alt="Poland" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/1/12/Flag_of_Poland.svg/23px-Flag_of_Poland.svg.png" width="23" height="14" class="thumbborder" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/1/12/Flag_of_Poland.svg/35px-Flag_of_Poland.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/1/12/Flag_of_Poland.svg/46px-Flag_of_Poland.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="1280" data-file-height="800" /></a></span> <a href="/wiki/Wroc%C5%82aw" title="Wrocław">Wrocław</a>, <a href="/wiki/Lower_Silesian_Voivodeship" title="Lower Silesian Voivodeship">Lower Silesian Voivodeship</a>, Poland, since 1963</li>

<li><span class="flagicon"><a href="/wiki/Czech_Republic" title="Czech Republic"><img alt="Czech Republic" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cb/Flag_of_the_Czech_Republic.svg/23px-Flag_of_the_Czech_Republic.svg.png" width="23" height="15" class="thumbborder" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cb/Flag_of_the_Czech_Republic.svg/35px-Flag_of_the_Czech_Republic.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cb/Flag_of_the_Czech_Republic.svg/45px-Flag_of_the_Czech_Republic.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="900" data-file-height="600" /></a></span> <a href="/wiki/Ostrava" title="Ostrava">Ostrava</a>, Czech Republic, since 1971</li>
